(See Probs. 15.1 to 15.9.)
15.4  Graphic Representation of Complex Numbers
The complex number x 1 yi may be represented graphically by the point P [(see Fig. 15.1(a)] whose rectangular 
coordinates are (x, y).
The point O having coordinates (0, 0) represents the complex number 0 1 0i 5 0. All points on the x axis 
have coordinates of the form (x, 0) and correspond to real numbers x 1 0i 5 x. For this reason, the x axis is 
called the axis of reals. All points on the y axis have coordinates of the form (0, y) and correspond to imaginary 
numbers 0 1 yi 5 yi. The y axis is called the axis of imaginaries. The plane on which the complex numbers are 
represented is called the complex plane.
In addition to representing a complex number by a point P in the complex plane, the number may be repre-
sented [see Fig. 15.1(b)] by the directed line segment or vector OP.
Fig. 15.1
15.5  Graphic Representation of Addition and Subtraction
Let z1 5 x1 1 iy1 and z2 5 x2 1 iy2 be two complex numbers. The vector representation of these numbers  
[Fig. 15.2(a)] suggests the familiar parallelogram law for determining graphically the sum z1 1 z2 5 (x1 1 iy1) 1 
(x2 1 iy2).
Since z1 2 z2 5 (x1 1 iy1) 2 (x2 1 iy2) 5 (x1 1 iy1) 1 (2x2 2 iy2), the difference z1 2 z2 of the two com-
plex numbers may be obtained graphically by applying the parallelogram law to x1 1 iy1 and 2x2 2 iy2. [See 
Fig. 15.2(b).]
In Fig. 15.2(c) both the sum OR 5 z1 1 z2 and the difference OS 5 z1 2 z2 are shown. Note that the segments 
OS and P2P1 (the other diagonal of OP2RP1) are equal.
	
(See Prob. 15.11.)
15.6  Polar or Trigonometric Form of Complex Numbers
Let the complex number x 1 yi be represented [Fig. 15.3(a)] by the vector OP. This vector (and hence the 
complex number) may be described in terms of the length r of the vector and any positive angle u which the 
vector makes with the positive x axis (axis of positive reals). The number r 5 Ïx2 1 y2 is called the modulus 

158
CHAPTER 15  Complex Numbers
or absolute value of the complex number. The angle u, called the amplitude of the complex number, is usually 
chosen as the smallest positive angle for which tan u 5 y/x, but at times it will be found more convenient to 
choose some other angle coterminal with it.
From Fig. 15.3(a), x 5 r cos u and y 5 r sin u; then z 5 x 1 yi 5 r cos u 1 ir sin u 5 r(cos u 1 i sin u). 
We call z 5 r(cos u 1 i sin u) the polar or trigonometric form and z 5 x 1 yi the rectangular form of the  
complex number z. An abbreviated notation is sometimes used and is written z 5 r cos u.
Fig. 15.3
EXAMPLE 15.5  Express z 5 1 2 iÏ3 in polar form. [See Fig. 15.3(b).]
The modulus is r 5 Ï(1)2 1 (2Ï3)2 5 2. Since tan u 5 y/x 5 2Ï3y1 5 2Ï3, the amplitude u is either 1208 
or 3008. Now we know that P lies in quadrant IV; hence, u 5 3008 and the required polar form is z 5 r(cos u 1 i sin u) 5 
2(cos 3008 1 i sin 3008). Note that z may also be represented in polar form by z 5 2[cos(3008 1 n3608) 1 i sin(3008 1 
n3608)], where n is any integer.
EXAMPLE 15.6  Express the complex number z 5 8(cos 2108 1 i sin 2108) in rectangular form.
Since cos 2108 5 2Ï3y2 and sin 2108 5 21/2,
z 5 8(cos 2108 1 i sin 2108) 5 8f2Ï3y2 1 2i(1y2)g 5 24Ï3 2 4i
is the required rectangular form.
	
(See Probs. 15.12 and 15.13.)

CHAPTER 15  Complex Numbers
15.7  Multiplication and Division in Polar Form
Multiplication
The modulus of the product of two complex numbers is the product of their moduli, and the amplitude of the 
product is the sum of their amplitudes.
Division
The modulus of the quotient of two complex numbers is the modulus of the dividend divided by the modulus 
of the divisor, and the amplitude of the quotient is the amplitude of the dividend minus the amplitude of the 
divisor. For a proof of these theorems, see Prob. 15.14.

15.8  De Moivre’s Theorem
If n is any rational number,
	
[r(cos u 1 i sin u)]n 5 r n(cos nu 1 i sin nu)	
A proof of this theorem is beyond the scope of this book; a verification for n 5 2 and n 5 3 is given in Prob. 15.17.

15.9  Roots of Complex Numbers
We state, without proof, the theorem: A complex number a 1 bi 5 r(cos u 1 i sin u) has exactly n distinct nth roots.
The procedure for determining these roots is given in Example 15.9.
EXAMPLE 15.9  Find all fifth roots of 4 2 4i.
The usual polar form of 4 2 4i is 4Ï2(cos 3158 1 i sin 3158), but we shall need the more general form
4Ï2 [cos(3158 1 k3608) 1 i sin(3158 1 k3608)]
where k is any integer, including zero.
Using De Moivre’s theorem, a fifth root of 4 2 4i is given by
	
{4Ï2[cos(3158 1 k3608) 1 i sin(3158 1 k3608)]}1/5	
	
5 s4Ï2d1y5 1cos3158 1 k 3608

1 i sin3158 1 k3608

5 Ï2 [cos(638 1 k728) 1 i sin(638 1 k728)]
	
Assigning in turn the values k 5 0, 1, 2, . . . , we find
	
k 5 0: Ï2(cos 638 1 i sin 638) 5 R1	
	
k 5 1: Ï2(cos 1358 1 i sin 1358) 5 R2	
	
k 5 2: Ï2(cos 2078 1 i sin 2078) 5 R3	
	
k 5 3: Ï2(cos 2798 1 i sin 2798) 5 R4	
	
k 5 4: Ï2(cos 3518 1 i sin 3518) 5 R5	
	
k 5 5: Ï2(cos 4238 1 i sin 4238)	
	
5 Ï2(cos 638 1 i sin 638) 5 R1, etc.	
Thus, the five fifth roots are obtained by assigning the values 0, 1, 2, 3, 4 (i.e., 0, 1, 2, 3, . . . , n 2 1) to k.
	
(See also Prob. 15.19.)
The modulus of each of the roots is Ï2; hence these roots lie on a circle of radius Ï2 with center at the origin. The 
difference in amplitude of two consecutive roots is 728, hence the roots are equally spaced on this circle, as shown in 
Fig. 15.4.

  15.7	 Find x and y such that 2x 2 yi 5 4 1 3i.
Here 2x 5 4 and 2y 5 3; then x 5 2 and y 5 23.
  15.8	Show that the conjugate complex numbers 2 1 i and 2 2 i are roots of the quadratic equation x2 2 
4x 1 5 5 0.
For x 5 2 1 i: (2 1 i)2 2 4(2 1 i) 1 5 5 4 1 4i 1 i2 2 8 2 4i 1 5 5 0.
For x 5 2 2 i: (2 2 i)2 2 4(2 2 i) 1 5 5 4 2 4i 1 i2 2 8 1 4i 1 5 5 0.
Since each number satisfies the equation, it is a root of the equation.
  15.9	 Show that the conjugate of the sum of two complex numbers is equal to the sum of their conjugates.
Let the complex numbers be a 1 bi and c 1 di. Their sum is (a 1 c) 1 (b 1 d)i and the conjugate of the sum 
is (a 1 c) 2 (b 1 d)i.
The conjugates of the two given numbers are a 2 bi and c 2 di, and their sum is
	
(a 1 c) 1 (2b 2 d)i 5 (a 1 c) 2 (b 1 d)i

15.17	 Verify De Moivre’s theorem for n 5 2 and n 5 3.
Let z 5 r (cos u 1 i sin u).
For n 5 2: z2 5 [r (cos u 1 i sin u)][r(cos u 1 i sin u)]
	
5 r2[(cos2 u 2 sin2 u) 1 i(2 sin u cos u)] 5 r2(cos 2u 1 i sin 2u)
For n 5 3: z3 5 z2 ? z 5 [r2(cos 2u 1 i sin 2u)][r(cos u 1 i sin u)]
	
5 r3[(cos 2u cos u 2 sin 2u sin u) 1 i(sin 2u cos u 1 cos 2u sin u)]
	
5 r3(cos 3u 1 i sin 3u)
The theorem may be established for n a positive integer by mathematical induction.

APPENDIX 1
Geometry
A1.1  Introduction
Appendix 1 is a summary of basic geometry definitions, relations, and theorems. The purpose of this material 
is to provide information useful in solving problems in trigonometry.
A1.2  Angles
An angle is a figure determined by two rays having a common endpoint. An acute angle is an angle with a 
measure between 0 and 90º. A right angle is an angle with a measure of 90º, while an obtuse angle has a measure 
between 90 and 180º. When the sum of the measures of two angles is 90º, the angles are complementary. When 
the sum of the measures of two angles is 180º, the angles are supplementary. T­wo angles are equal when they 
have the same measure.
Fig. A1.1
If two lines intersect, the opposite angles are vertical angles. In Fig. A1.1(a), ∠AED and ∠BEC are vertical 
angles and ∠CEA and ∠BED are also a pair of vertical angles. When two angles have a common vertex and 
a common side between them, the angles are adjacent angles. In Fig. A1.1(b), ∠PSQ and ∠QSR are a pair of 
adjacent angles. If the exterior sides of two adjacent angles form a straight line, the angles form a linear pair. 
In Fig. A1.1(c), ∠WXZ and ∠ZXY are a linear pair.
Properties and Theorems
s	 The measures of vertical angles are equal.
s	 The angles in a linear pair are supplementary.
s	 If the angles in a linear pair are equal, the angles are right angles.
s	 Angles complementary to the same or to equal angles are equal to each other.
s	 Angles supplementary to the same or to equal angles are equal to each other.

169
APPENDIX 1  Geometry
A1.3  Lines
Two lines in a plane either intersect or are parallel. If two lines intersect, they have exactly one point in common. 
Two lines in a plane are parallel if they have no common point.
When two lines intersect to form equal adjacent angles, the lines are perpendicular. Each of the angles 
formed by two perpendicular lines is a right angle. The sides of a right angle are perpendicular.
A transversal is a line that intersects two or more coplanar lines in distinct points. In Fig. A1.2, lines m and 
n are cut by transversal t. When two lines are cut by a transversal, the angles formed are classified by their loca-
tion. The angles between the two lines are called interior angles and the angles not between the two lines are 
called exterior angles. Interior or exterior angles are said to alternate if the two angles have different vertices 
and lie on opposite sides of the transversal. A pair of corresponding angles are two angles, one an interior angle 
and one an exterior angle, that have different vertices and lie on the same side of the transversal. In Fig. A1.2, 
the interior angles are numbered 3, 4, 5, and 6 while the exterior angles are numbered 1, 2, 7, and 8. The angles 
numbered 3 and 6 and the angles numbered 4 and 5 are pairs of alternate interior angles. The angles numbered 
1 and 8 and those numbered 2 and 7 are pairs of alternate exterior angles. The pairs of corresponding angles are 
numbered 1 and 5, 2 and 6, 3 and 7, and 4 and 8.
	
Fig. A1.2
Properties and Theorems
s	 In a plane, if two lines are perpendicular to the same line, then the lines are parallel.
s	 Two lines parallel to a third line are parallel to each other.
s	 If two parallel lines are cut by a transversal, then the alternate interior angles are equal.
s	 If two parallel lines are cut by a transversal, then the alternate exterior angles are equal.
s	 If two parallel lines are cut by a transversal, then the corresponding angles are equal.
s	 If two parallel lines are cut by a transversal, then the interior angles on the same side of the 
transversal are supplementary.
s	 Any pair of horizontal lines are parallel.
s	 Any pair of vertical lines are parallel.
s	 Any vertical line is perpendicular to any horizontal line.
s	 If two angles have their sides parallel, right side to right side and left side to left side, then the 
angles are equal.
s	 If two angles have their sides perpendicular, right side to right side and left side to left side, then  
the angles are equal.
s	 If two lines are cut by a transversal so that the alternate interior angles formed are equal, then the 
lines are parallel.
s	 If two lines are cut by a transversal so that the alternate exterior angles formed are equal, then the 
lines are parallel.
s	 If two lines are cut by a transversal so that the corresponding angles formed are equal, then the lines 
are parallel.

170
APPENDIX 1  Geometry
s	 If two lines are cut by a transversal so that the interior angles on the same side of the transversal  
are supplementary, then the lines are parallel.
s	 If a transversal is perpendicular to one of two parallel lines, it is perpendicular to the other one as well.
s	 Through a point not on a line there is exactly one line parallel to a given line.
s	 Through a point not on a line there is exactly one line perpendicular to a given line.
s	 In a plane, there is exactly one line perpendicular to a given line at any point on the line.
A1.4  Triangles
A triangle is a plane closed figure formed by three line segments that intersect each other at their endpoints. 
Triangles that have no two sides with the same length are called scalene triangles, those with at least two sides 
having the same length are called isosceles triangles, and those with all three sides having the same length are 
called equilateral triangles. If a triangle contains a right angle, it is a right triangle. A triangle that does not 
contain a right angle is called an oblique triangle.
Two triangles are said to be congruent when they have the same size and the same shape. When two triangles 
are congruent, the pairs of corresponding sides have the same length and the pairs of corresponding angles are 
equal. Triangles that have the same shape are said to be similar. Similar triangles have corresponding sides that 
are proportional in length and corresponding angles that are equal.
A median of a triangle is a line segment from a vertex to the midpoint of the opposite side. An altitude of a 
triangle is a line segment from one vertex perpendicular to the opposite side.
Properties and Theorems
s	 If three sides of one triangle are equal to the three sides of another triangle, the triangles are 
congruent.
s	 If two sides and the included angle of one triangle are equal to the corresponding two sides and 
included angle of another triangle, the triangles are congruent.
s	 If two angles and the included side of one triangle are equal to the corresponding two angles and 
included side of another triangle, the triangles are congruent.
s	 If two angles and a nonincluded side of one triangle are equal to the corresponding two angles and 
nonincluded side of another triangle, the triangles are congruent.
s	 If the hypotenuse and leg of one right triangle are equal to the corresponding sides of another right 
triangle, then the two right triangles are congruent.
s	 If two angles of one triangle are equal to the corresponding two angles of another triangle, the 
triangles are similar.
s	 The sum of the measures of the angles of a triangle is 180º.
s	 An exterior angle of a triangle is equal to the sum of the measures of the two nonadjacent interior 
angles of the triangle.
s	 The acute angles of a right triangle are complementary.
s	 The measure of each angle of an equiangular triangle is 60º.
s	 If two sides of a triangle are equal, then the angles opposite these sides are equal.
s	 If a triangle is equilateral, then it is also equiangular.
s	 If two angles of a triangle are equal, then the sides opposite these angles are equal.
s	 If a triangle is equiangular, then it is also equilateral.
s	 The altitude to the base of an isosceles triangle bisects the base and the vertex angle.
s	 The median to the base of an isosceles triangle bisects the vertex angle and is perpendicular to the base.

171
APPENDIX 1  Geometry
s	 The bisector of the vertex angle of an isosceles triangle is the perpendicular bisector of the base.
s	 In a right triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse c is equal to the sum of the squares of 
the lengths of the two legs a and b; i.e., c2 5 a2 1 b2 (Pythagorean theorem).
s	 In a 45º–45º right triangle, the length of the hypotenuse c is equal to Ï2 times the length of a leg a; 
i.e., c 5 Ï2a.
s	 In a 30º–60º right triangle, the length of the hypotenuse c is equal to 2 times the length of the leg a 
opposite the 30º angle; i.e., c 5 2a. Also, the length of the leg b opposite the 60º angle is equal to 
Ï3 times the length of the leg a opposite the 30º angle; i.e., b 5 Ï3a.
s	 The midpoint of the hypotenuse of a right triangle is equidistant from all three vertices of the triangle.
s	 If the square of the length of one side c of a triangle is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of 
the other two sides a and b of the triangle, i.e., c2 5 a2 1 b2, then the triangle is a right triangle.
s	 The area K of a triangle is ­one-­half the product of its altitude a and base b; i.e., K 5 1
2ab.
s	 The area of an equilateral triangle is equal to ­one-­fourth of the square of a side s times Ï3; 
i.e., K 5 1
4s2Ï3.
s	 If the sides of a triangle are unequal, then the angles opposite them are unequal in the same order.
s	 If the angles of a triangle are unequal, then the sides opposite them are unequal in the same order.
A1.5  Polygons
A polygon is a plane closed figure whose sides are line segments that are noncollinear and each side intersects 
exactly two other line segments at their endpoints. A quadrilateral is a polygon having four sides. A regular 
polygon is a polygon that is both equilateral and equiangular. A diagonal of a polygon is a line segment that 
joins two nonadjacent vertices of the polygon.
A parallelogram is a quadrilateral with opposite sides parallel. A rectangle is a parallelogram with one 
right angle. A rhombus is a parallelogram with two adjacent sides equal. A square is a rectangle with two 
adjacent sides equal.
A trapezoid is a quadrilateral with exactly one pair of parallel sides. An isosceles trapezoid is a trapezoid 
with the nonparallel sides having equal lengths.
Properties and Theorems
s	 The opposite angles of a parallelogram are equal.
s	 The opposite sides of a parallelogram are equal.
s	 The diagonals of a parallelogram bisect each other.
s	 The consecutive interior angles of a parallelogram are supplementary.
s	 The sum of the interior angles of a quadrilateral is 360º.
s	 If both pairs of opposite angles of a quadrilateral are equal, the quadrilateral is a parallelogram.
s	 If both pairs of opposite sides of a quadrilateral are equal, the quadrilateral is a parallelogram.
s	 If the diagonals of a quadrilateral bisect each other, the quadrilateral is a parallelogram.
s	 A rectangle is an equiangular parallelogram.
s	 A rhombus is an equilateral parallelogram.
s	 The diagonals of a rectangle are equal.
s	 The diagonals of a rhombus are perpendicular.
s	 If the diagonals of a parallelogram are equal, the parallelogram is a rectangle.
s	 If the diagonals of a parallelogram are perpendicular, the parallelogram is a rhombus.
s	 The diagonals of a square are the perpendicular bisectors of each other.

172
APPENDIX 1  Geometry
s	 A rhombus with a right angle is a square.
s	 A square is a regular polygon.
s	 The diagonals of an isosceles trapezoid are equal.
s	 The area K of a parallelogram is equal to the product of its altitude a and base b; i.e., K 5 ab.
s	 The area K of a rectangle is equal to the product of its length l and width w; i.e., K 5 lw.
s	 The area K of a rhombus is equal to ­one-­half the product of its diagonals d and d; i.e., K 5 1
2dd.
s	 The area K of a square is equal to the square of its side s; i.e., K 5 s2.
s	 The area K of a square is equal to ­one-­half the square of its diagonal d; i.e., K 5 1
2d2.
s	 The area K of a trapezoid is equal to ­one-­half the product of the altitude h and the sum of the  
bases b and b9; i.e., K 5 1
2h(b 1 b9).
A1.6  Circles
A circle is the set of all points in a plane that are at a given distance from a given point. Any line segment which 
has its endpoints on a circle is a chord of the circle. If a chord of a circle passes through the center of the circle, 
then it is a diameter. A radius is a line segment from the center of a circle to a point on the circle. A secant is a 
line that intersects a circle in two points. A tangent is a line that intersects a circle in one point.
An arc of a circle is a part of the circle from one point on the circle to another. A semicircle is an arc of a 
circle joining the endpoints of a diameter of the circle. An inscribed angle is an angle with sides that are chords 
of a circle. A central angle is an angle with sides that are radii of the circle.
Properties and Theorems
s	 A circle contains 360º.
s	 A semicircle contains 180º.
s	 A central angle is equal in degrees to its intercepted arc.
s	 An inscribed angle is equal in degrees to ­one-­half its intercepted arc.
s	 An angle inscribed in a semicircle is a right angle.
s	 In a circle, if a diameter is perpendicular to a chord, it bisects the chord and its arc.
s	 In a circle, two chords that are equal are equidistant from the center of the circle.
s	 In a circle, two chords that are equidistant from the center of the circle are equal.
s	 A tangent to a circle is perpendicular to the radius drawn to the point of tangency.
s	 If a triangle is inscribed in a semicircle, then the triangle is a right triangle.
s	 In a plane, if a line is perpendicular to a radius of a circle at its endpoint on the circle, then the line is 
tangent to the circle.
s	 The line segment joining an external point to the center of a circle bisects the angle formed by the 
two tangents to the circle from that point.
s	 The lengths of the two tangent segments from an external point to a circle are equal.
s	 If two secants intersect in the interior of a circle, then the angle formed is equal in degrees to 
­one-­half the sum of the arcs intercepted by it and its vertical angle.
s	 If a line through the center of a circle bisects a chord that is not a diameter, then it is perpendicular to 
the chord.
s	 The circumference C of a circle is equal to p times the diameter d; i.e., C 5 pd.
s	 The radius r of a circle is equal to ­one-­half the diameter d; i.e., r 5 1
2d.
s	 The area K of a circle is equal to p times the radius r squared; i.e., K 5 pr2.­
